Okta's CEO is betting big on AI agent identity | Decoder

Decoder with Nilay Patel(11.2K) ·60:40 ·1.3K views · 2026-03-30
Nilay Patel (Host, Decoder) Todd McKinnon (CEO, Okta)
Okta CEO Todd McKinnon is pivoting the company to become the primary identity and security layer for AI agents, betting that the "Agentic Enterprise" will expand the software market far beyond the current scope of human-centric SaaS.
TL;DR Okta CEO Todd McKinnon is pivoting the company to become the primary identity and security layer for AI agents, betting that the "Agentic Enterprise" will expand the software market far beyond the current scope of human-centric SaaS. Healthy Paranoia: McKinnon...

TL;DR

Okta CEO Todd McKinnon is pivoting the company to become the primary identity and security layer for AI agents, betting that the "Agentic Enterprise" will expand the software market far beyond the current scope of human-centric SaaS.

The "Sasspocalypse" and Market Expansion

  • Healthy Paranoia: McKinnon acknowledges the threat of "vibe coding" (customers building their own tools with AI) but argues that enterprise software's true value lies in the "last mile"—thousands of integrations, 100% uptime, and a brand reputation that boards trust during a breach.
  • Capturing the Services Budget: The total addressable market (TAM) is growing because AI allows companies to shift labor budgets (currently a ~$1.8T IT services market) into software budgets (~$1.2T).
  • The "Pie" Logic: While some legacy SaaS tools may be disrupted, McKinnon believes the overall technology pie is expanding significantly, similar to the shifts seen during the internet and cloud revolutions.

Strategy: The Blueprint for Agentic Identity

  • The New Primary Identity: Okta is moving to treat AI agents as a distinct identity type—a hybrid between a human user and a system service.
  • The Three Pillars of the Blueprint:
      • Onboarding & Inventory: Providing enterprises with a centralized "system of record" for every agent running across their network, whether built internally or provided by vendors like Salesforce or Microsoft.
      • Standardized Connections: Moving toward an industry standard for how agents share permissions and access data across different software "silos."
      • The "Kill Switch": A security fail-safe that allows an admin to instantly revoke an agent’s access to all company data if it exhibits non-deterministic or "rogue" behavior.
  • The Revenue Opportunity: McKinnon predicts that managing agentic identity could eventually become the largest single category in the $280B cybersecurity industry.

Leadership and Organizational Agility

  • The "Change Quotient": To adapt to 2026’s software landscape, McKinnon has pushed Okta to turn up its "dial of change." While a normal company might aim for 20% change and 80% stability, he is mandating a 60/40 split to force innovation.
  • Decision Framework:
      • Filtering Decisions: McKinnon focuses on "deciding which decisions to make," delegating smaller choices to maintain a high success rate on high-stakes strategic pivots.
      • Trusting Instincts: After years of slowing down for "expert advice," McKinnon is returning to founder-driven instincts to move faster in a volatile market.
  • Top-Down Mandates: While preferring a bottom-up culture, McKinnon argues that massive technical shifts (like the pivot to AI agents) require top-down leadership to overcome organizational inertia.

The Future of Infrastructure and Regulation

  • The "Unbundling" of Software: Apps are being split into three layers: Data, Intelligence, and Interface. Okta aims to be the secure "connective tissue" between these layers.
  • Antitrust Concerns: McKinnon suggests that if dominant players (like Microsoft or Amazon) use "monopolistic lock-in" to prevent third-party agents from accessing their data silos, government intervention or antitrust enforcement may be necessary to protect customer choice.
  • Fighting AI Fraud: To counter AI-powered scams and bots, Okta is betting big on the digitization of "offline" identities, such as mobile driver’s licenses and biometric passports, as the ultimate verification layer for "real" humans.
"Winning [the identity layer for AI agents] is job number one for our company... this new agent layer could be the biggest category in cyber by far." — Todd McKinnon
"No one's ever maintained an agentically developed system for five years. No one's ever figured out how to make it scale... what’s going to happen is the software engineers are going to be figuring out how to make it work at scale." — Todd McKinnon
"If the market doesn't work, if customers can't force the choice... then we have to rely on regulators to come in and fix it." — Todd McKinnon

You're casting them out amongst the gods. The... it's interesting. So when I started Okta, you know, I'd worked at Salesforce and I had a, you know, decent-sized team there and I felt like I was very decisive. I was like, "We got to do something. Here are the options. Decide." And then I started Okta and I found something interesting. My decision-making process slowed down. And when I thought about why, it's like I realized that when I was at Salesforce, my boss was always a safety net ultimately, right? It's like if I was going to make a bad decision, there was theoretically a boss to... to stop me. But when I started doing Okta and the company started getting successful, I was, you know, my decision was like *the* decision and I'd better think about it and get it right. And so it slowed down, it slowed down. And then the company got bigger and we got into this phase of... you know, we went public and got closer to a billion dollars revenue. And then I kind of felt like maybe I didn't... I needed more input and I really needed to get expert advice on a lot of things. And what I realized over those years is that my instincts were still pretty good and I probably should trust my instincts more, and... and so I think that's kind of the mode I've been in for the last three years. Yeah, it's the company's bigger than it's ever been. I'm managing a company that's bigger than I've ever managed by definition, but I think I've been leaning into my... my instincts more. And I think to inform those... and then to put more, you know, detail on that, I think two things are very important. One is that you... you have to decide which decisions to make. So that's really important. So there's a bunch of decisions that I shouldn't be involved in and I shouldn't be making. Why Smart Ideas Don’t Always Land — and How to Build Creative, Curious Teams

World Economic Forum(1.1M) ·25:29 ·509 views · 2026-03-30
Paul Hoffman (CEO, Liberty Science Center)
> "People who do out-of-the-box stuff, it's not by accident...
TL;DR: In an era of AI-generated content, the primary competitive advantage for leaders is the ability to filter ideas, challenge foundational assumptions, and translate complex concepts into irresistible, human-centric narratives. The "Jargon Wall": Innovation is often...

TL;DR: In an era of AI-generated content, the primary competitive advantage for leaders is the ability to filter ideas, challenge foundational assumptions, and translate complex concepts into irresistible, human-centric narratives.

Radical Clarity: Breaking Down Professional Barriers

  • The "Jargon Wall": Innovation is often stifled by experts who use exclusionary language. Effective leaders must ensure that "serious academic content" is accessible to a broad audience without being "watered down."
      • The Gut Check: Hoffman advises leaders to never let a sentence slide if they don’t personally understand it. Many professionals avoid asking for clarification to avoid "looking dumb," which results in flawed communication and execution.
      • Intellectual Honesty: At *Encyclopædia Britannica* and *Discover* magazine, the standard was to assume the audience is smart but possesses zero prior knowledge of the specific subject.
  • The Power of Narrative: Complex data becomes "visceral" when anchored in story.
      • Example: At the Liberty Science Center, the "Sue the T. rex" exhibit (a 90% complete skeleton) focuses on the animal's life story—broken ribs from fights with Triceratops and a jaw infection—rather than just dry measurements.

Audience-Centric Leadership and Service Mindsets

  • The "Doorman" Philosophy: Hoffman’s early experience as a doorman taught him that leadership is fundamentally a customer service role. Putting the audience’s needs first is essential for engagement.
  • Real-World Feedback Loops:
      • Observational Research: Hoffman frequently walks the floor of the Liberty Science Center in casual clothes to observe where visitors get stuck or excited.
      • Engagement Metrics: In the museum world, the "audience is always right"—if they walk past an exhibit in five seconds, the design or messaging has failed, regardless of the internal team's intent.
  • Unlocking Arcane Subjects: During his time as a chess commentator for ESPN, Hoffman focused on the "human tension" and psychological drama of the players to engage viewers who didn't understand the technicalities of the game.

Challenging Assumptions to Drive Fresh Thinking

  • The Puzzle Master’s Approach: Problem-solving is most effective when leaders systematically identify and overturn "old basic assumptions" in their industry.
      • Example (Medical Innovation): For decades, the medical community assumed ulcers were caused by stress. It took a researcher (Barry Marshall) challenging that "Freudian world view" by drinking bacteria to prove they were actually caused by *H. pylori*.
  • Iterative Ideation: Avoid the "Perfect Pitch" trap. Hoffman encourages informal, early-stage discussions rather than requiring teams to spend 40 hours on a polished deck that might be rejected in five minutes.
  • Brainstorming Dynamics:
      • Safe Spaces: Leaders must prevent "pouncing" on ideas early in the process. The goal of brainstorming is to capture all ideas without immediate judgment.
      • Diverse Teams: High intelligence is less important than team chemistry and the ability for members to "play off each other."

Modeling Curiosity and Exploration

  • Leadership Accessibility: To foster a curious culture, leaders must be "fairly casual" and "very accessible." Frequent, informal interactions lower the stakes for employees to share unconventional ideas.
  • Curiosity Killers:
      • Authoritarianism: Leaders who are poor listeners or appear too authoritative dampen their team's willingness to think outside the box.
      • Intellectual Isolation: Cutting off ideas too quickly signals a lack of curiosity to the entire organization.
  • External Inputs: Creativity requires a "broad perspective" gained through activities outside the office.
      • Active Exploration: Hoffman advocates for long-distance urban walking (8-10 miles) and spending time in nature to reset the mind.
      • Intergenerational Connection: Staying in touch with younger generations (e.g., Hoffman’s 26-year-old) and attending art performances helps maintain a contemporary perspective.

"A leader that's just, you know, not a good listener and not understanding the passions and interests of the people around him... that is a curiosity killer." — Paul Hoffman

So when I've worked with companies on brainstorming, I work with them on trying to understand whatever their oldest basic assumptions were in their industry or their product, right? And looking at them and seeing what would happen if you overturned some of them, even if it seems crazy. At least it's a thought exercise to do that. I mean, we're so often blinded by what we bring that nobody questions. Sometimes it's cultural things. I'll just give you a broad example. You know, for years it was thought that ulcers stemmed from a mental condition. In other words, like an unhealthy mind leads to an unhealthy body. So if you had ulcers, it was because you had too much stress in your life. You know, it's turned out that the vast majority of ulcers are caused by a bacteria and have nothing to do with stress at all. But it was very hard for researchers to discover that because they were all under this sort of Freudian world view that mental stress would cause bodily harm. And in fact, when the first researcher came up with the idea that most ulcers or many of them anyway were caused by bacteria, the medical community didn't believe him. And he stood there on the stage of a conference and drank a beaker of—or a test tube—of the bacteria that he said caused ulcers. Sure enough he came down with ulcers and then showed he could take an antibiotic and—I mean, that was an extreme thing to do. But just that for decades we were blinded to even considering the possibility that the, you know, "troubled mind leads to ulcers" might not be correct. I mean, that kind of thing is prevalent all over the place. You need to isolate what your assumptions are and then do the thought exercise, "Well, what if, what if I'm wrong about that?" and see where that leads. What other things can sort of help with brainstorming? Where does it go sideways? What can they do to sort of give themselves the best shot at having the best discussion? I mean, you want to create an atmosphere where people aren't pouncing on each other and criticizing each other's ideas. I mean, you need people to be able to feel a comfort level with their colleagues that they can say stuff, maybe it's silly. Okay, then move on to another subject if that doesn't spark something interesting. But you need to create sort of a safe space where, particularly initially in brainstorming, you're not being judgy about stuff. When instead whoever the facilitator is is trying to capture all the ideas. It's like an anonymous, you know, Google Doc or something like that where people can just put whatever in there without any sort of fear of reprisal or even a name. Is that sort of thing where they kind of get a thought started together, is that helpful to get people's thoughts moving? That could be. I mean, I did a lot of things in groups. I did a lot of things with talking to people individually. Because sometimes a person who's shyer in a certain setting—because I might walk into something in a company where I don't know the history of the dynamics between the individuals in the room. So I didn't come in as a corporate psychologist. I'm not there to like solve dysfunctional things, but I'm in there to try to get people for them to work together to be more creative and succeed. And a lot of it has to do with the team dynamics. I mean, you know, some people think that you need to get the 12 smartest people together and that's going to lead to the brightest ideas. That's not true at all. You need a team that plays off of each other and works well together. And how can leaders promote curiosity? By demonstrating it themselves, right? If a leader cuts him or herself off to ideas too quickly, that's not expressing any curiosity. They need to be curious about their employees, their employees' lives, and of course about their joint work together. Are there overlooked curiosity killers, things that people maybe don't realize they're doing it, but it's actually sort of dampening people's ability to think outside the box? You know, a leader that's just, you know, not a good listener and not understanding the passions and interests of the people around him. I mean, someone who comes across as too authoritative. It's a leader's job in the end to make hard decisions and all that. But you really need to set up a space where people can be playful without fear of repercussions. How do you do that at Liberty Science Center? My style is fairly casual, and I'm also very, very accessible. And that's part of it. So the people I work with, I mean, see me often. It's not like they say, "Oh my God, I'm not going to see him for six months, I've got to prepare and I'm going to blow our chance to get this new thing going unless we really impress him in that five minutes." No. I run into people constantly. You know, I like to start with talking about things in an informal way. I don't want people to spend 40 hours doing the perfect pitch. Why don't we start with something and then if it seems interesting then go off and develop it. I mean, that's something where leaders have to be conscious of. If you have people putting in like oodles of work to get something to a point and then you shoot it down in five seconds, that's not great. Of course, they've put a lot of effort and time into it. You need to set up a system where you're engaging with your team and people early enough so that the work that they are doing is and ending up being something that you're going to use and they feel good about. I'm a, you know, lived there my whole life. But I also like to get out in nature. That's important. Even if it's for just short periods of time. Where do you walk these days? And how does it help you sort of tend to your curiosity and your creativity? Because I just see a lot, you know. I'm exposed to stuff. I mean, that's the thing. You should just go out and do stuff. You know, I go to a lot of art openings, a lot of performances and things. Stay in touch with younger people. I have a 26-year-old, so it helps. NVIDIA GTC Studio with Insights from Vertiv

NVIDIA(2.2M) ·12:05 ·1.4K views · 2026-03-30
Vertiv provides the critical power and cooling infrastructure necessary to support high-density AI workloads, transitioning from bespoke data center construction to a "converged infrastructure" model that uses digital twins and pre-fabricated blocks to accelerate deployment.
TL;DR Vertiv provides the critical power and cooling infrastructure necessary to support high-density AI workloads, transitioning from bespoke data center construction to a "converged infrastructure" model that uses digital twins and pre-fabricated blocks to accelerate...

TL;DR

Vertiv provides the critical power and cooling infrastructure necessary to support high-density AI workloads, transitioning from bespoke data center construction to a "converged infrastructure" model that uses digital twins and pre-fabricated blocks to accelerate deployment.

The Foundational Role of Infrastructure

  • The "Five-Layer Cake" Analogy: Scott Armul identifies Vertiv as the foundational infrastructure layer of the AI ecosystem. Without this foundation—which provides the physical power and thermal management—the upper layers of the AI stack cannot function.
  • Power and Cooling Demands: AI GPUs generate extreme heat and require massive energy draws. Vertiv’s core mission is delivering that power and then removing the heat from the chip, the rack, and the building, often moving toward liquid cooling or heat reuse to increase efficiency.
  • From Background to Foreground: Previously considered a background player in data centers, Vertiv’s role has become highly visible as power and cooling have become the primary bottlenecks to AI scaling.

Digital Twins and the NVIDIA Omniverse Partnership

  • Reference Design Announcement: Vertiv announced a one-core solution for the NVIDIA Omniverse/DGX reference design, providing an end-to-end, pre-designed system for full data centers.
  • Physics-Based Simulation: By using NVIDIA Omniverse, Vertiv can simulate the performance of infrastructure—including power trains and thermal chains—in a digital environment before any physical construction begins.
  • Accelerated Deployment: Digital twins allow companies to move "from shovel to token" faster by identifying performance issues and interface points digitally, reducing the time spent on-site for commissioning and troubleshooting.

The Shift to Converged Physical Infrastructure

  • Beyond Bespoke Construction: Traditional data centers are often built as "bespoke," custom on-site projects where various components from different vendors are integrated for the first time in the field.
  • Standardized Building Blocks: Converged Infrastructure replaces custom builds with pre-defined block sizes and standardized interface points.
      • This approach treats the data center more like a "factory" than a construction site.
      • Pre-fabricated systems ensure that all components are designed to work together from the start, lowering the Total Cost of Ownership (TCO).

Scaling for Next-Generation AI Factories

  • Gigawatt-Scale Ambitions: Armul notes that the industry is moving toward "AI Factories" that operate at gigawatt capacity, requiring a radical rethink of traditional power density assumptions.
  • Future-Proofing for GPUs: Vertiv is looking multiple GPU generations ahead to solve "physics-based problems" before they arise, such as:
      • Delivering power to racks exceeding 600kW.
      • Optimizing the site to function as a "single computer" rather than a collection of independent servers.
  • Compounding Acceleration: The roadmap for AI performance is accelerating, necessitating "maniacal" focus on technology investments to ensure infrastructure can keep pace with chip architecture.
"We've gone from important to the data center and the AI industry but relatively unknown... to folks who are actually paying attention to who we are and what we do now." — Scott Armul
"Converged building blocks and this converged approach to design ideally makes us look and feel a lot more like a factory [than a construction site]." — Scott Armul
"We are the infrastructure layer... the most important layer because it's the foundation of everything that eventually gets built on top." — Scott Armul
Hi everyone. Welcome to the NVIDIA GTC Studio. My name is Tiffany Janzen and today I am joined with a very special guest, Scott Armul, from Vertiv. Scott is the chief product and technology officer. Scott, how are you today? Doing well. Thank you very much for having me. Thank you for being here. Scott, I have to admit something. When I saw that we were having a sit-down conversation, I I have been really looking forward to this because what you are doing at Vertiv is solving a really big challenge with AI and infrastructure that I think—I mean, I know you know this for many, many years now—but I think the general public is starting to become aware of. I think that's fair. From a Vertiv perspective, I think we've we've gone from important to the data center and the AI industry but relatively unknown to folks who are actually paying attention to who we are and what we do now. Vertiv, we're the power and cooling and overall solutions infrastructure folks from a data center level. We're helping to actually build and fabricate and design the data centers of the future. The Mining Paradox: Record Low CapEx Just Created A Generational Buy, Here's Why | Tavi Costa

Kitco NEWS(766.0K) ·46:03 ·68.5K views · 2026-03-30
The Federal Reserve is "trapped" by massive sovereign debt and cannot raise rates effectively to combat a 1970s-style commodity shock, creating a generational buying opportunity in hard assets and mining equities that remain undervalued despite record-high metal prices.
TL;DR The Federal Reserve is "trapped" by massive sovereign debt and cannot raise rates effectively to combat a 1970s-style commodity shock, creating a generational buying opportunity in hard assets and mining equities that remain undervalued despite record-high metal prices. ...

TL;DR

The Federal Reserve is "trapped" by massive sovereign debt and cannot raise rates effectively to combat a 1970s-style commodity shock, creating a generational buying opportunity in hard assets and mining equities that remain undervalued despite record-high metal prices.

The Fed’s "Debt Trap" and Persistent Inflation

  • The Fiscal Constraint: Tavi Costa argues the Fed cannot tighten financial conditions significantly because interest payments as a percentage of GDP are approaching a critical 4% to 6% threshold, requiring the economy to grow at that same rate just to service debt.
  • 1970s vs. Today: While the 1970s saw double-digit rate hikes to curb inflation, the current levels of public debt (reaching 200% in historic precedents like the British Empire) make such moves impossible without imploding the private equity and banking sectors.
  • The "PhD Lens" Fallacy: Costa criticizes policymakers for using abstract CPI models that ignore real-world pressures.
      • Energy Impact: Official models suggest energy price spikes only impact CPI by 3%, while for the bottom 50% of the population, energy and food often represent 15% to 25% of their total budget.
  • Policy Direction: Expect "higher for longer" inflation with suppressed interest rates. The government will likely use currency debasement or debt restructuring to manage the load, both of which are highly inflationary.

The Mining Paradox: Record Low CapEx

  • Structural Supply Deficit: Despite gold and copper reaching historic highs, the mining industry is experiencing record-low capital expenditure (CapEx) and exploration budgets.
  • The Investment Gap:
      • Long Lead Times: Projects started today will not provide supply for 5 to 7 years.
      • Lack of "Boldness": Management teams, scarred by decades of low prices, remain too conservative to initiate a major M&A or production wave.
  • Government Intervention: The US government is reportedly planning to spend $200 billion to build critical mineral reserves. Costa notes this single player's budget is larger than the entire current mining industry’s market cap, suggesting a massive future re-rating.
  • Valuation Divergence: Mining represents only ~1% of global equities today, compared to historical peaks of 15%.

Gold as the "Hard Settlement" Asset

  • The Shift from Paper: The global financial system is moving from "paper claims" to "hard settlement." Gold is re-emerging as the ultimate settlement asset of last resort, potentially becoming more relevant than the US Treasury market.
  • Central Bank Behavior:
      • Turkey Gold Sales: Costa dismisses reports of Turkey selling gold as a "one-off" emergency to defend the lira, rather than a systemic trend.
      • Reserve Diversification: Countries like China, India, and Brazil continue to revamp balance sheets with gold to reduce dollar dependency.
  • Portfolio Protection: Institutional investors (pension funds and family offices) have yet to fully rotate into gold, representing a massive wave of "unleashed" demand still to come.

Actionable Strategy and Specific Picks

  • Buying the Dip: Costa views recent pullbacks as a "gift" for stock pickers. He focuses on three criteria: high-quality assets, proven management teams, and tight capital structures.
  • Specific Equities:
      • Orla Mining: Cited for its high-quality team and institutional backing (e.g., Pierre Lassonde).
      • Aura Minerals: Highlighted for its tight capital structure (40-50% family-owned) and strong management under CEO Rodrigo Barbosa.
  • Strategic Allocation:
      • Quality Mid-Tiers: Investing in companies that have the potential to become the "seniors" of the next cycle.
      • Contrarian Treasury Hedge: Costa maintains a small Treasury position as a tactical hedge against a self-inflicted recession, despite his long-term inflationary thesis.
      • Energy as a Hedge: Recommends owning energy exposure to offset the rising fuel costs that typically hurt mining margins.
"The biggest ballistic missile we're having here in the US economy is not Iran, it is rates going higher... the system simply cannot withstand it." — Tavi Costa
"I would challenge anybody to show me one time in history that the mining cycle ended with CapEx at all-time lows. It’s always the other way around." — Tavi Costa
"Gold is becoming probably one of the most important assets in the world... it is going to become more meaningful than even the US Treasury market." — Tavi Costa
Welcome back, I'm Jeremy Szafron. Gold is hammering the $4,500 level, oil is pinned above $110, and the central bank is quietly admitting it cannot control the supply shocks driving both. Jerome Powell recently noted at Harvard that the Fed has limited ability to counter these types of war-driven disruptions while arguing that longer-term inflation expectations remain anchored. Now the bond market has rallied and traders are aggressively pricing out rate cuts. This comes as shipping through the Strait of Hormuz remains severely disrupted, leaving the market to deal with a major supply shortfall. The hard asset complex is responding, but not in a simple way. Spot gold is catching a strong bid while silver pushed past that $70 mark. Now it is a market wrestling with inflation risk and growth fear at the exact same time. Powell says stay calm. Tavi Costa says the system is trapped, and the market is trying to decide which one is right. Now joining us to cut through the noise is Tavi Costa, of course founder and CEO of Azuria Capital. Marc Andreessen: Will a16z Go Public & Why Labour Displacement with AI is Wrong?

20VC with Harry Stebbings(195.0K) ·76:33 ·19.9K views · 2026-03-30
Marc Andreessen (Co-Founder and General Partner, Andreessen Horowitz) Harry Stebbings (Host, 20VC)
Marc Andreessen argues that the narrative of AI-driven labor displacement is fundamentally incorrect, asserting instead that current tech layoffs are a correction for massive corporate overstaffing.
TL;DR Marc Andreessen argues that the narrative of AI-driven labor displacement is fundamentally incorrect, asserting instead that current tech layoffs are a correction for massive corporate overstaffing. He reinforces that Silicon Valley has reached its highest level of...

TL;DR

Marc Andreessen argues that the narrative of AI-driven labor displacement is fundamentally incorrect, asserting instead that current tech layoffs are a correction for massive corporate overstaffing. He reinforces that Silicon Valley has reached its highest level of centralization in history due to the AI boom, while maintaining that a16z has no current need to go public.

The Venture Capital Mindset: Omission vs. Commission

  • The "Scalded Stove" Fallacy: Andreessen warns against "learning from mistakes" in venture capital. If an investor loses money in a sector (e.g., search in the 90s or AI in the 80s), they often develop an emotional bias against that category, leading to "mistakes of omission."
      • Priority of Risk: In venture, the cost of a failed $10M investment (commission) is negligible compared to the $100BN opportunity cost of missing the next Google (omission).
  • Identifying "Greatness" in Founders: Andreessen utilizes a three-part formula for evaluating early-stage talent:
      • High IQ: This is "table stakes." He tests this by whether he is compelled to take notes during a pitch.
      • Courage: The absolute determination to "embrace the suck" and pound through obstacles.
      • Primal Drive: An intrinsic ambition to build, often (though not always) rooted in childhood "brokenness" or trauma that creates a necessity for success.
  • Investment Philosophy: Andreessen follows Arthur Rock’s conclusion that one should invest 100% on the "resume" (the person) rather than the business plan. A great team will always lap a mediocre team with a perfect plan.

AI and the Myth of Labor Displacement

  • The Productivity Argument: Andreessen rejects the "lump of labor" fallacy, stating that labor displacement theories are 100% incorrect. Technology increases the marginal productivity of the individual worker; for example, AI allows coders to be more productive, which typically leads to them working *more* because the value of their output has increased.
  • Corporate Overstaffing: Current industry layoffs are attributed to two factors unrelated to AI capabilities:
      • Interest Rates: The jump from 0% to 5% forced a total re-planning of corporate cost structures.
      • The COVID Hiring Binge: Andreessen estimates that most large companies remain overstaffed by 25% to 75%. AI is currently being used as a "silver bullet excuse" for layoffs that were actually necessitated by a loss of hiring discipline in 2020-2021.
  • Consumer Surplus: He predicts that 99% of AI's economic value will accrue to consumers (users) rather than the companies building the models. He compares this to the internet and smartphones, where the vast majority of value is "dark matter" that benefits global productivity rather than just corporate balance sheets.

Firm Strategy and the Future of a16z

  • The "Cake Baking" Phase: a16z views the first two years of a startup as the period where the "sugar" is added to the cake. If the core formula is wrong at the seed stage, it cannot be fixed with later capital.
  • On Going Public: Andreessen states there is nothing a16z is missing today that would be solved by going public. He notes that dealing with Limited Partners (LPs) is far preferable to the pressures of public markets, famously stating that unlike hedge fund managers, LPs "aren't shorting your stock."
  • Valuation and "Diamonds in the Rough":
      • He cautions that more companies die from "indigestion" (overfunding) than starvation.
      • However, he admits that every time a16z passed on a "promising venture company" solely over price, it was a mistake.
      • He advises against looking for "diamonds in the rough," arguing that if a deal is actually good, other smart VCs will already be bidding on it.

Geopolitics and the Silicon Valley Monopoly

  • Recentralization: While Andreessen hoped COVID-era remote work would decentralize tech, he observes that the industry is now more concentrated in Silicon Valley than ever before. He notes that nearly 100% of "quality" AI companies are located within a 20-mile radius of Palo Alto.
  • The "Europe Problem": Andreessen identifies a lack of "courage" and a preference for "managerialism" in European policy. While the human capital in Europe is world-class, the regulatory environment and lack of risk appetite stifle growth.
      • Reflexive Backing: He suggests a strategy of reflexively backing any European founder who moves to the U.S., as the move itself acts as a filter for high risk-tolerance.
  • Emerging Hubs: He expresses significant optimism regarding the "courage" and ambition currently seen in the UAE and Saudi Arabia, noting their willingness to execute on large-scale technological shifts.
"The tech industry is more centralized in Silicon Valley than it has been in its entire existence. And I think it's AI." — Marc Andreessen
"This entire labor displacement thing is 100% incorrect. It's completely wrong. Essentially, every large company is overstaffed... by at least 25%... I think a lot of them are overstaffed by 75%." — Marc Andreessen
"Life just gets a lot simpler if you just assume everything is your own fault... it gives me ownership of the problem and it gives me something that I can do." — Marc Andreessen

But finally today, I'm proud to have Marc Andreessen on the show. I think every time we passed on a promising venture company over price, I think it's been a mistake. There's nothing that we're missing today that we could solve by going public. The man who has built one of the greatest firms of our time, they manage over $90 billion. The tech industry is more centralized in Silicon Valley than it has been in its entire existence, and I think it's AI. And they have invested in some of the most generational companies. This entire labor displacement thing is 100% incorrect. It's completely wrong. Essentially, every large company is overstaffed. It's at least overstaffed by 25%. I think most large companies are overstaffed by 50%. I think a lot of them are overstaffed by 75%. This was a very special one for me, and I hope you enjoy the episode. Ready to go? Marc, you probably don't know this, but I started this show when I was 18 years old and you were one of three names that I wanted to have on the show back in 2015. I have to admit, I've ticked off the other two and so I'm a bit worried that I'm going to have to stop after doing this show. But I'm so touched that you agreed to join me. So thank you for joining me. Good. I'm thrilled to be here. Now, I was running and listening to every show that you've done before, and you recently said that you don't introspect and introspection is potentially overrated. I really struggled with this because I thought we learned from mistakes and I valued experience in that way. Can you help me understand the lack of value placed on introspection and do we not learn from mistakes? You know, we do learn from mistakes, but the problem is learning from mistakes sometimes is good and sometimes is bad, right? And if I just talk business for a moment, like in the venture mindset, this is a very big problem. There's a founder version of the mistake and there's a venture version of the mistake. The founder version of the mistake is if a founder starts a company in a category and the founder doesn't work, the founder is then emotionally angry at that category for the rest of his life and will not acknowledge when there's something that's going to work in that category. And I've just seen that like over and over and over again. And that's fine, because most founders go on to do other things and that's fine and good, and it generally doesn't damage them from a business standpoint. In venture, the same thing happens. If you invest in a category, or if you invest in a kind of company, or you invest in a kind of founder and it doesn't go well, it's extremely easy to learn from the mistake, right? And to basically say, "All right, I touched that hot stove. I'm never doing it again." And then, you know, you can tell me what happens next, right? Which is the next thing shows up and pattern matches and it's the thing that you should invest in and you have the chance to invest in. But you touch this scalded stove and you know you're, you know, you're learning from your mistakes, right? You're doing the responsible thing and so you don't do it. And so I think there's something that's particularly pernicious about learning from your mistakes in venture capital. And then I think that's also somewhat true about life. You get married multiple times. As they say, it's the triumph of hope over experience. Like I think probably you want hope to triumph over experience in that domain. And I think there's a lot of other domains of life in which that's probably true. I totally understand what you're saying, especially when you say about, "Hey, it's easy to lose money in a sector and then think the sector by nature is cursed, or it's so difficult, you can't make money in healthcare, you can't make money in X." I'm old enough to remember internet search. Like, you can't make money in internet search. Like the internet search companies in the 1990s did not work out well. So then how do you, when you are guiding conversation, when you are guiding partners, how do you ensure that they have a fresh mind with every new company and every new investment and are not plagued by the downsides that bluntly lost money before? Yeah, so and by the way, just the other example, by the way, is AI. AI was a tremendously good way to lose a lot of money in venture capital from 1945 to 2017, right? So there are, I mean, look, when I was getting my computer science degree in the late eighties, like AI was like the one field that you knew would never succeed. Like, there had actually been an investment boom for AI in the eighties and it failed. And everybody, including all the computer scientists, were like, "Yeah, this field is dead." And that happened like five times over the course of AI over the last 80 years. And so like that's just again, another great example. So look, I think a couple things in terms of how we run our firm, or how you run a firm like this. So one is, as you well know, there are very important—there are two categories of mistakes, right? There's the mistake of commission and there's the mistake of omission. Or there's the mistake of cost and there's the mistake of opportunity cost. And so of course, the mistake of cost is you invest $10 million in a startup, it fails, you lose the money. You lose the money. That's bad. The mistake of omission is you don't invest in Google and you lose a hundred billion dollars of opportunity cost, right? And so of course, venture is like the most polarized. It's the most polarized possible economic field in which this is true. And by the way, if you're running a, you know, and if you're running a bond business or something, you know, debt business or something where you can't—you just can't lose money or the whole thing doesn't work, then obviously you can't run in this kind of model. In that case, you better learn from your mistakes. But in venture, right? In venture, I think you're always much more worried about the mistake of omission than you are worried about the mistake of commission. And then to your question, like, I think in a lot of ways that's the key thing that Ben and I do at this point in our lives in our roles at the firm is we're not like micromanaging the investment decisions of the firm. And we have, I think, spectacular senior partners and junior partners that are doing a great job of that. And we're in the room for it and so forth. But like, we're generally not advocating for or against a deal. But what we are trying to do is to get everybody to constantly have this risk forward, worry about the mistake of omission over the mistake of commission mindset, this anti-scalded stove phenomenon. You know, we just routinely remind people, like, yeah, you're emotional about this because of your bad experience three years ago or six years ago or 10 years ago and just like let that go. You no longer have to pay for that sin and you're completely liberated to be able to kind of let that emotion, you know, fade into the distance and be able to focus on the opportunity in front of you. My biggest regret or omission experience is one of your companies, actually. It's ElevenLabs. We could have invested at the seed round, but we would have only got 1%, Marc. And naturally, as an emerging manager, I thought it was important to retain the high ownership model I promised my LPs. How do you reflect or advise me on when to break the rules versus when to maintain doing what I said I would do? So, you know, quite honestly, it's the simplest answer in the world and it's the hardest answer in the world. And it's the answer that I think every great investor ends up resolving to 30 years in, frankly. I actually had this discussion actually with Arthur Rock, who's sort of the, obviously the virtually the creator of modern venture capital. And he actually wrote a paper on this topic, and I'll just give you his conclusion, which is also my conclusion. Arthur Rock, for people who don't know, he invested in Apple and Intel right in the seed rounds and in many, many other great companies for like 30 years. His conclusion was that he would have been a better venture investor had he fed all of the business plans and pitch decks straight into the shredder upon receiving them and if he had spent 100% of his time on the resume. And I think that's basically right, which is the great founders will basically buy you enormous upside that may break rules in all kinds of directions and may break precedent in all kinds of directions. And the world's best business plan executed by a mediocre team will almost certainly get lapped by a great team. Now, let me say having said that, this sounds easy, of course. Why is that hard? It's because it's somewhat tautological, right? Because we define great founders as the ones that have great outcomes. And so it's a lot easier after the fact to be able to say, "Oh yeah, Steve Jobs was a great founder" when you look at the success of Apple. But nevertheless, I think that is the answer, which is when you have special people, you should back them almost basically almost without consideration of other factors. And when you don't, you shouldn't. And at the end of the day, it like that simply is the core thing. My personal formula is basically as follows, which is you need high IQ as table stakes. Like you just need somebody who's like incredibly smart. My basic test is if I have my notebook open and they're talking, am I like writing down lots of notes or not? So I believe—and and we try to run the firm this way—I believe the core of the business is—is a permanent state of affairs. And it's—and it—the core of the business is early stage. Like that really fundamentally is the core of the business. You know, look, a metaphor we use all the time at at the firm is is it—it's like startups are like baking a cake. If you leave the sugar out of the cake—like if you—if you bake the cake and you leave the sugar out of the cake, you can't pour sugar on the cake afterwards and fix your mistake, right? Right, like sugar has to go in the cake. And so that first two years is when you're baking the cake. Like that first two years when you're really figuring out what the formula is of what you're doing and what the product is and what the company is and what the business is and what the culture is and who the team is—like those decisions are like absolutely fundamental. And if you get those right as a founder, like the payoff from that will go for decades. And if you get those wrong, like even if your company succeeds, you're going to live with those sins forever. They're going to extrapolate out. And so like it—like that's it. Like that is the core of the business. And like many great companies, you know, later on bring in lots of other partners and growth-stage investors and, you know, add other people to the team and build boards and it's fantastic and it's great. Right, because you know, because you build this incredible emotional bond and then, you know, you have like complete context on why all the decisions got made and you, you know, you remember how it first started. By the way, you remember how hard it was. But there's—there's another really fundamental thing where we decide—why we decided to go so big in growth on on that front, which is—this is less true now, but it, you know, 10 years ago, 15 years ago, these companies would raise money from venture investors and then they would get to a certain point and then they would raise money from a completely different kind of investor that was not tech-centric. And then they would all of a sudden end up in this situation where they had s—you know, s—sort of a conflict between investment mentalities, a—a—a fundamental conflict on the cap table over things like, you know, level of risk, level of reinvestment, you know, level of you know, when—how fast they should go public, whe—if they should sell the company, do they need to replace the founder, bring in a professional CEO? You know, the—if you bring in a—if you bring in a—to the extent you bring in non-tech mentality, non—whatever you want to call it Silicon Valley mentality, growth-stage investors, you do set yourself up for like—you're now going to get a different set of pressures. And so one of the things we wanted to be able to do is to be able to, you know, with—with our founders that have the chance to build something really great, like we want to be able to be their partner across, you know, potentially every round that they do. And then as a consequence of that, you know, they can basically preserve our mentality on their cap table for longer and longer and longer. And and I think that that works pretty well. Is it possible to literally—I love what you said there because I love the boutique craftsman style of venture. But is it—is it literally possible to care about a $5 million seed check when you have $15 billion that you raise at once? Yes. And it—it is, and the reason for that—twofold: one is just the—the—you know, the conceptual kind of reasons that I described. But the other is just purely on pure economics it is. But—but I think there's a second phase to it, which again I'm like very excited about. And the second phase, which relates to your—your new question. The second phase is I think the benefits of AI, the power of AI diffuses out globally like—like to a degree people are really not expecting. And furthermore, I think that's already happening. And I think—but this is also an answer, by the way, to your inequality question because you know the—the sort of assumption always is, "Well you know, surely the biggest companies in the world will have access to the best technology or the rich people will have access to the best technology or whatever like that." And it's actually quite striking if you look at AI, it's—I think it's the most hyper-democratic, small-d democratic technology I think we've ever seen. And it follows kind of the internet and follows smartphones in this, which is why I'm pretty confident that this is what's going to happen. I think it's already happening, which is the best AI in the world is the app that you download on your iPhone off the App Store. Right? Like the best AI in the world is OpenAI or whatever, whichever one it is of the, you know, three, four, five that are really in the race, you download that app, that's the best AI. Right? And by the way, look, you—to your inequality point—like you're probably going to have to pay 20 bucks for it and then if you really use it a lot, you're going to have to pay 200 bucks for it. And so I think that's such a hyper-democratization of the technology and so I think the use of AI, like the—the consumer benefit, the business benefit, the economic benefit, I think that has the potential to be decentralized to a radical degree. Let me pause there and then I'll answer your question of what this means as an investor. I guess the question is really to what extent do we feel it is a just assessment that the models will move into the infra—move into the application layer and erode value? You know, we saw obviously Anthropic announce security update—I'm using that as an example because it's ridiculous in my eyes. They announce security update and CrowdStrike and Cloudflare tank 8-9%. Obviously, it's not threatening CrowdStrike and Cloudflare today. Do you think the core models AI, OpenAI, for example, will move continuously into the application layer and consume more and more of the value chain? Yeah. So a couple things. So one is there's a bigger phenomenon which is what I was heading towards in my earlier answer. So there's a bigger—there's actually an even bigger phenomenon than that. And there's actually a paper on this, maybe we can link to it, and it uses the—the term Schumpeterian economics after—after Joseph Schumpeter who's the kind of, you know, the economist who kind of developed the theory of creative destruction. And this economist basically goes through and says there's a concept of Schumpeterian economics, Schumpeterian gains, and the idea of it basically is—and he does this whole analysis for a whole bunch of different technologies. And basically, when there's a new fundamental technology, whether it's electricity, you know electricity or steam power or computers or the internet or smartphones or, you know, AI, what actually ends up happening is like something close to 99% of the economic value arrives in the market not in the form of economic benefit to the companies that make the thing, but rather to the customers. And the economists call this consumer surplus. Right? And so it's—it's all of the benefit that the consumers are getting that they're not actually—that they're not fully paying for. And so anyway, the way this analysis basically works is if you look at the total amount of economic value creation, for example, downstream of the internet, something like 99% of that accrued to the users of the internet, not the companies that built the internet. Right? Same thing with the smartphone, right? Who gets the economic value of the smartphone? The—everybody in the world who uses a smartphone to become more productive in their life or in their business gets 99% of the value from the smartphone. Apple and Google get 1% of the value from the smartphone. I think AI is the exact—I think it's already that way. I think it's going to be exactly the same way. It might even be greater than that. It might be 99.9999% of the value of AI is going to accrue to the users, not to the companies that make the AI. And I just bring that up because like—I think that's like such a larger economic force. That's such a larger amount of value that's just like extending out into the world that, like I said, it's—it's almost like dark matter. It's like every—everybody's going to experience that in their own life and in their own business that they run and everything that they build, you know, wherever they are in the world and they're using AI and, you know, and nobody's ever going to really like tally that up or get credit for it. But if you do the analysis, it's going to turn out where that's like overwhelmingly where the gains are. So as a consequence, on your—so your question is basically a question of then fighting for like the 1% that stays captured, you know, kind of in the AI industry itself. Which is a very important question, and of course, it's central to what we... Well, I guess the question is, does that whole economic theory change when we believe that we will see the labor being eaten? So this entire labor displacement thing is 100% incorrect. It's completely wrong. It's classic zero-sum economics. It's the lump of labor fallacy. It happens over and over and over again. It's always been wrong. It's going to be wrong again. "Do you even believe it for mediocre people? And I know that sounds very judgmental and horrible, but most social media managers, Marc, are crap. Which is there's a certain amount of work to be done and either the machines do it or the humans do it and so surely those jobs go away. They're going to be able to use AI to become more productive at work. And again, this has happened many, many times. You take an individual worker who used to write on pencil and paper and you give them a typewriter. Oh, oh, that's very easy. So number one, interest rates. So as you know, so interest rates—interest rates—interest rates were at zero. The God Machine Demis Hassabis and the Quest for Superintelligence | Sebastian Mallaby

Hidden Forces(65.6K) ·58:56 ·930 views · 2026-03-30
Demetri Kofinas (Host, Hidden Forces) Sebastian Mallaby (Author and Guest) Demis Hassabis (Co-founder, Google DeepMind)
Demis Hassabis is driven by a quasi-spiritual compulsion to advance science through Artificial General Intelligence (AGI), transitioning from a "singleton" vision of safe, collaborative development to a high-stakes competitive arms race against OpenAI.
TL;DR Demis Hassabis is driven by a quasi-spiritual compulsion to advance science through Artificial General Intelligence (AGI), transitioning from a "singleton" vision of safe, collaborative development to a high-stakes competitive arms race against OpenAI. Demis Hassabis’s...

TL;DR

Demis Hassabis is driven by a quasi-spiritual compulsion to advance science through Artificial General Intelligence (AGI), transitioning from a "singleton" vision of safe, collaborative development to a high-stakes competitive arms race against OpenAI.

The Architect of the "Infinity Machine"

  • Demis Hassabis’s Background: A child chess prodigy and polymath, Hassabis developed a conviction to build superintelligence as early as 1993, long before AI could perform basic tasks like image recognition.
      • The "100% Mode": Co-founder Shane Legg describes Hassabis as having no "50% or 99% mode"; his life is entirely consumed by the mission to build AI, eschewing hobbies for relentless scientific pursuit.
      • Neuroscience as a Blueprint: Before founding DeepMind, Hassabis earned a PhD in neuroscience to understand the "existence proof" of intelligence—the human brain—to better replicate it in silicon.
  • The "Sweetness of Discovery": Mallaby compares Hassabis to Robert Oppenheimer, noting that the "sweetness" of scientific breakthrough often compels creators to move forward despite known existential risks.
      • Philosophical Motivation: Hassabis views AI as a tool to understand the fundamental nature of reality, describing his late-night research sessions in quasi-spiritual terms as a quest to understand why the universe is "set up" to be intelligible.

Technical Paradigm: Deduction vs. Induction

  • The "Infinity Machine" Concept: The book’s title refers to the shift from symbolic AI (deductive logic) to deep learning (inductive pattern recognition).
      • Deductive Limitations: Traditional "logic-based" programming failed because human intelligence and natural categories (like "what makes a dog a dog") are too messy for hard-coded rules.
      • Inductive Breakthrough: Modern AI thrives on processing an "infinity" of data to intuit patterns through trial and error, a process where the machine—not the programmer—discovers the underlying code for tasks like facial recognition.
  • The Language Model Pivot: DeepMind initially de-emphasized Large Language Models (LLMs), believing language was just a system of symbols ungrounded in reality.
      • The Transformer Catalyst: While OpenAI immediately pivoted to LLMs after the 2017 Transformer paper, DeepMind remained focused on "agentic" systems (like AlphaGo) until GPT-3’s performance in 2020 forced a strategic correction.

Strategic Consolidation: The Google Acquisition

  • Avoiding the VC Treadmill: DeepMind was sold to Google in 2014 primarily because Hassabis found fundraising from Venture Capitalists (like Peter Thiel) a distraction from science.
      • Access to Compute: The acquisition provided the "unlimited" hardware and financial resources needed to hire top-tier scientists and run massive compute-heavy experiments.
  • Vetting the Tech Titans: Hassabis reportedly "tested" Mark Zuckerberg by discussing augmented reality; he concluded Zuckerberg didn't grasp that AI was orders of magnitude more important, leading him to choose Google’s Larry Page instead.
  • Founder Dominance: Despite having co-founders (Mustafa Suleyman and Shane Legg), Hassabis held 90% of the initial shares, allowing him to dictate the company’s trajectory with minimal internal resistance.

The Collapse of the "Singleton" Theory

  • The Race Dynamic: Hassabis originally hoped for a "singleton scenario" where one lab would develop AGI on behalf of humanity to ensure safety. This vision was shattered by the rise of OpenAI and the aggressive commercialization of the technology.
      • The "Bare-Knuckle Fight": The release of ChatGPT in 2022 was viewed by DeepMind as a "betrayal" of safety norms, parking "tanks on the front lawn" and forcing a competitive sprint that compromised cautious development.
  • Safety vs. Competition: Mallaby highlights a recurring theme: an intelligent, well-meaning person attempting to build "safe" AI, only to have every theoretical safety framework (like the singleton model) dismantled by market and geopolitical pressures.
"When you see a technology that is sweet, you go ahead and invent it and you worry about the consequences afterwards." — Demis Hassabis (quoting Robert Oppenheimer)
"There's no 50% mode in Demis, there's no 99% mode in Demis, it's always more than 100% all of the time." — Shane Legg (as told to Sebastian Mallaby)
"If I understand the main existence proof that you can even have intelligence—the human mind—then I will understand the endpoint of where AI is trying to get to." — Demis Hassabis
"I want both [the practitioner and the larger issue]. I don't want to do an abstract argument with no actual protagonists in it, nor do I want to do protagonists and character just for the sake of character. It's got to mean something for how the world works." — Sebastian Mallaby
Sebastian Mallaby, welcome back to Hidden Forces. So great to be here. It's great to see you, though not in person this time. How you been? Oh, I've been having a great time. We're going to discuss it, but to be embedded inside an AI lab right when ChatGPT gets released is pretty good. So this is what, is this like your fifth book, your sixth book? I've lost track. This is number six. Yep, I'm a repeat offender. And you've been on the show two previous times. The first time you were on was way back in the early days of the program, and you had just published a book on Alan Greenspan's career, his life and times. And then you came back on to discuss *The Power Law*, which was a book on the venture capital industry. Almost all your previous books, with maybe the exception of *After Apartheid*, seem to be either primarily biographies—*The Man Who Knew*, which I just mentioned, and this latest book, *The Infinity Machine*, qualify—or historical accounts of institutions or industries in which key figures play a defining role, such as with *More Money Than God* and *The Power Law*. But even in this book and in your biography of Alan Greenspan, the main character is used to tell a much bigger story. What is it about you, your interests, your curiosities, your nature, the questions that make you feel alive and that align with your life's mission and purpose, that might explain to someone looking from the outside why you've chosen to write the books that you've written? Well, I'm always looking for, you know, the story, the practitioners, the real people, but also the larger issue. I want both. I'm greedy. I don't want to choose. I don't want to do an abstract argument with no actual protagonists in it, nor do I want to do protagonists and character just for the sake of character. It's got to mean something for how the world works. So all of my books have those two tracks running in parallel. And, you know, I find that to be not only fun to write, maybe fun to read, but actually also, that's where the truth is, right? Because in the way the real world—well, you know, we have this debate about: is it a great man theory of history or a great person theory of history or are there ineluctable deep forces, as a Marxist historian would suggest? You know, and I'm firmly of the view that the truth is in the middle. There's a mixture. Sometimes, you know, something like demographics, technological change, you know, can drive what happens. Other times there really are individuals who make decisions and determine outcomes. And so you need to capture both in order to do a good book. So it seems that you've undergone a shift in focus from finance to technology in the last decade of your life. Do you, does that track? I mean, I'm going off of obviously the subject matters of your book, but if so, what explains that shift in focus, do you think? It does track, and I'd say it's explained by two things. One is just my curiosity. I always want to find something new to get my teeth into intellectually. But the other thing is that the world itself has moved in this direction. If you think about the period before the 2008 financial crisis, you know, the sort of sense of where the energy was in capitalism was often in finance, you know, in kind of the rise of hedge funds, the rise of private equity. You know, if you thought about the phrase "Masters of the Universe," that was a reference to Wall Street. So I guess my writing interests have followed the way the world has changed, and AI is a great example of that. In fact, the populist outrage that we felt post-2008 directed at the banking system now feels like it is being redirected in Silicon Valley. Do you, does that track also with your experience? Yeah, I think that Silicon Valley sometimes has a slight upper hand in that, you know, the products, the consumer products, people tend to like them. I mean, they complain about them, of course. And although they may wish they didn't do quite so much time on Instagram, they're kind of happy that they've got, you know, messages, they're in touch with their friends, they've got a map that, you know, avoids losing your way in a different city, you can call an Uber, all that stuff. So basically people have direct contact with the products and they like them. So I think the sort of animus against finance is in some ways deeper. How are the tech founders and pioneers different from the financiers and economists that you've written about in the past? Well, that's a good question. I mean, I think tech is fundamentally more powerful and is driving in fact a lot of the changes in finance. If you think about, you know, I had a very interesting conversation about a month or two ago with somebody who's a veteran Wall Street insider. And I mean, we were talking about, you know, why did private equity take off in the 1980s? And his story was, well, it was the arrival of the personal computer on trading floors and the sort of first spreadsheet software, which enabled you to do valuation models of private companies and then figure out, whoops, these private companies are super cheap compared to the public ones. And nobody realized that until technology came along. Or if you think about securitization or, you know, any number of financial products, it's technology that often advances and enables that, and then of course quantitative hedge fund trading and electronic market making and all this stuff. So I think at the root of most change in capitalism, there is technological change. And so in this sense, the tech sector is more powerful and also given correspondingly to more hubris. So how would you say the individuals are different? How are they different intellectually? How are they different constitutionally, emotionally? Do you find that there are certain aggregate behaviors or archetypes that track between these groups? You know, that's a great question. I mean, I'd say that, you know, that the stereotype would be that the Wall Street titan is some guy in an expensive suit and shiny shoes who sort of shakes your hand a bit too firmly and, you know, is a kind of connector, networker, schmoozer, boaster. Whereas the archetypal tech titan might be some, you know, computer guy who prefers to write code than to talk to people. And so I think, you know, that's the stereotype. I think it's way too simple. I mean, in my experience, if you look at finance, you've got both the hedge fund tribe who can be quite introverted because it's sort of numbers on a screen, you don't really need to talk to people and you just make up your mind individually how you're going to trade. Versus venture capitalists, where it's all about, you know, connections and you've got to persuade the human being who is the entrepreneur to bond with you so that you get to invest in that company, not somebody else. Well, that's a good point because I remember again, I read *The Man Who Knew* and it's not the only account of Greenspan that I've read and of course I grew up as a young man still when Greenspan was chairman of the Federal Reserve. So, as I mentioned, this book is called *The Infinity Machine: DeepMind and the Quest for Superintelligence*. How did you go about convincing Hassabis to participate in this biography? What kind of access did he give you, and were there any conditions that he demanded in order to work with you on this project? Sure. Well, I went to see him, you know, proving that it's better to be lucky than smart. I went to see him in November of 2022. And I made a pitch to him, which was basically, you know, I'd before I went to see him, I listened to every single podcast or lecture or whatever he'd done. So I knew the way he talked and I knew his belief system. And clear in his beliefs was the idea that AI was going to be the most important invention in human history. And I said, look, if this is true and you're the leading creator of it, that means you're one of the most important people in human history. And he goes, oh, I guess. So all of our worldview and the way we live is going to be upended, and it's going to be upended partly by you, and people have a right to know: who are you? What is your value system? Why are you doing this, right? What motivates you to do something which is so disruptive? And I would check quotes with him, so he had the right to veto a quote, but I could use the information. So, you know, I could relay to the reader the truth that he conveyed to me. And that, you know, put him in the spotlight a lot. Did this project require you to become conversant or knowledgeable in certain domains? Well, I mean, that's the fun of it, right? I mean, all of my books I try to get really deep in the subject matter and to really understand it in a way which, you know, hasn't been done before in the kind of literature which is aimed at the general reader. So, you know, that was true of hedge funds, that was true of central banking, and that was true of, you know, how venture investing really works, how you generate alpha in that way. You know, I but I'd say the dominant in the end in this project, the dominant two ways I learned about how to think about AI at a kind—not at a matrix mathematical matrix multiplication level, but at a kind of conceptual level: what is deep learning? What is reinforcement learning? How do they interact? What's the difference between the transformer model and the seq2seq model that came earlier, which was another way of trying to map sequences of data onto each other? Based on those papers on arXiv, tell me what's striking about this person's work compared to the person I interviewed last week. And so dialoguing with large language models was quite productive too. Did you ever feel out of your depth, so much so that it limited what you would have liked to be able to get out of an interview or out of a case study? Sure. I mean, you always feel, and that's again, the fun of it, you always feel stretched. And so by definition there are moments when you have to say to somebody, I didn't understand that. Can you just explain that again? And in some ways that can actually be productive because, you know, when somebody explains it the second time, they may be come up with a better analogy to explain it, or, you know, they they kind of make it more colorful by putting in some anecdote about how they struggled with this point but then they had an insight that got them past it and then that insight itself becomes a story in the book. So I think, you know, you just have to be honest with the people you're talking to and say, hey, I'm, as you know, I'm not technical. I have tried to educate myself, but I'm going to interrupt you now and again and say can you explain that. So I think it's, you know, if you've got the time, these books take me three, four years and so I do have the time to go really deep with people. To speak to them for a long time, I prepare for each meeting for like a week or something, so you go in really educated and then follow up with the same people, go back and see them for another two hours.
