Do you, how do you feel lucky that you're able to, you've built a career that essentially allows you to be a perpetual student? And not only a perpetual student, but anyone in the world who's interesting or worth talking to, you can pretty much get a sit-down with that individual. Well, it's a huge privilege that I have had a lot of access to a lot of amazing people. And yeah, I'm curious. I love learning from them and, you, know, that also informs, I think somebody said to me, oh your books are kind of generous, you know, when you judge human nature, you're you're not it's not "gotcha." And that's true. I'm trying to explain what people are doing. I'm trying to get into their thought process, figure out which bits of it because when people explain themselves, they often don't know why they did something right. But you have to listen very carefully and then maybe you've talked to somebody else who's in an analogous job and they've had a better explanation. So it's a bit like, you, know, the tennis player McEnroe was asked: why is your topspin forehand so amazing? And then he couldn't explain it. But if you filmed that wrist and you then slowed it down, the slow-motion clips would show you exactly when he was rolling his wrist over forwards and so on. So you want to capture the wrist motion in the intellectual process of anybody you're writing about is essentially what I'm saying. And I find that explanatory task so much more satisfying than reaching a kind of slightly cheap and aggressive judgment about people. I mean, because because most of the people I write about, they're not evil people, they're certainly not dumb people. They're kind of trying to do their best, they make mistakes, but if you understand mistakes as sort of like, yeah, they were in a position where there was no good choice, it's just much richer and more interesting and more true to how the world works. Yeah, also if you're adversarial from the start, if you're adversarial from the get-go, it's difficult to get your interlocutor to trust you, but also you you have less credibility to push in areas where it's relevant because you, know, if you spend your whole interview pushing the individual, it's hard to differentiate between where it really matters and where it doesn't. We've spent quite a bit of time on this portion of the background because it's just especially interesting to me and hopefully to the audience. One more question before we get to the book: how did you decide to go to press with a book about something of civilizational importance like AI that is still in the process of playing out? Did you feel the temptation to continue refining the story and not release it? No. I mean, partly I thought there was a good landing place for the story, which is that, you know, I began my interviews right when ChatGPT was released and OpenAI became the leading lab in AI because of that model, overtaking Demis Hassabis and DeepMind for a bit. But then when I finished my writing at the end of 2025, Gemini 3, the the Google DeepMind model, was judged to be better than the OpenAI model. So I think there was a natural arc to that part of the story. Of course my book also tells a whole backstory about what was going on in AI before we get to ChatGPT. But I mean, I I felt there was a sort of satisfying resting place and of course the story's going to continue, we're going to get more agentic systems, we're going to get robotic systems, and and all of that's going to be super exciting. But I do think I've nailed one important slice of modern AI history. And I wanted to get it out because, frankly, we're just getting into the moment in 2026 where everybody is going to have their lives affected. We're just seeing the effects on the job market starting, I believe, and, you, know, when your when your life is turned upside down, you want to understand what's going on. It's like a bereavement or something. First, you know, you're in denial for a bit, but then you need to understand what happened. And I think reading an account of why it was created, who was the individual who created it, what motivated them, what was their values, this is actually quite important in terms of how people can process the AI that is disrupting their lives. So this book is predicated on the argument that in order to understand the inventors of transformational technologies, we need to understand their motivations, what makes them tick in other words. What is most important for us to understand about Demis Hassabis, the founder of DeepMind? Well, that in his case, the motivation for building AI, which he knows might well be dangerous, is a sort of compulsion to advance science, an absolute addiction to the idea that science has to progress. The sweetness of discovery. That's right. So the sweetness of discovery. This is a phrase that goes back to Robert Oppenheimer, the father of the Manhattan Project. And what he said was, you know, when you see a technology that is sweet, you go ahead and and invent it and you worry about the consequences afterwards. And this line was then quoted by Geoffrey Hinton. He's the AI professor who also got a Nobel Prize for inventing—or not quite inventing but developing—neural networks, deep learning. And so it's a phrase which is very kind of present in the AI world, that they are doing something equivalent to the atom bomb, inventing a technology that's dangerous, but it's so sweet you can't resist it. And I think Demis is drawn to that sweetness very powerfully, so much so that he sometimes describes it in quasi-spiritual terms. And Geoffrey Hinton famously resigned from Google in order to speak out against his concerns around AI risk, and that's something we're definitely going to have a chance to talk about. What is most important for us to understand about Hassabis's life, whether that be his early experiences as a chess prodigy, his time at Cambridge and University College London, and the importance he ascribes to certain movies and novels like *Ender's Game* or books like Hofstadter's *Gödel, Escher, Bach*? Well, Demis's life is extraordinary from start to finish. I mean, it really is one of the most amazing characters I've ever met in my long career of writing about people. And I mean, clearly the smartest as well. And so I think, you, know, if we just start with the early life because I think it was formative. One of the questions we have to ponder is why did somebody, you, know, living in London of all places, not even in Silicon Valley, develop this conviction that he was going to devote his life to building super powerful AI, right? He has this conviction starting around 1993. This is 15 years before AI can even recognize the photograph of a cat. Like AI could do nothing in 1993, but here is this kid, 17 years old, absolutely convinced that he's going to devote his life to building AI. Where does that come from? Where does the conviction come from? And I think you can only understand it if you understand that he was, you, know, the kid of immigrants. His mother was Chinese Singaporean, living in North London. His dad was of Greek extraction. And he was just outlandishly clever. And he would drop out of school for a year at a time because he was the captain of the junior chess team in Britain and he was focusing on chess and just sort of studying by himself in his bedroom. And so he was a total outsider. And, you, know, he was weird to his parents because he was so extraordinarily precocious with his brains. He was weird to his schoolmates when he even went to school because, you, know, here is this Chinese-looking kid who's like insanely clever and disappears and, you, know, makes a bunch of money by winning chess tournaments around the place. So it kind of came naturally that he developed an ambition which for anybody in 1997 was crazy, for somebody in London was completely crazy, right? I want to build powerful AI. He did it by, you, know, his chess led to, you, know, also he was very fascinated by coding. He got hired by a games company in building early video games. And he went off to stay in the house of the entrepreneur who had set up this gaming company. And this guy used to talk about AI and kind of slightly gave Demis this bug, this ambition. But it it had to—it came from a very unusual place. And so I think understanding, you, know, both how unusual it was his childhood, but also how driven it made you because chess was crazy competitive. They used to have these wooden planks underneath the tables because otherwise the contestants would kick each other in the shins to distract the other guy. It was vicious. And, you, know, people would break down, there would be tears all over the place, people lost games and, you, know, so it was brutal competition, but also very conducive to developing a kind of autodidact individualism. Yeah, his early experience as a chess prodigy was informative not just because he came to a determination at some point that it wasn't enough and that it was a waste of his brain power, but also there's this great part of the book where he recounts his father telling him that he had to basically give it his all and that would be enough. And what he heard was basically: push yourself to near-death. And that's the way you know for sure that you've given it your all. How important was that period as a kid, both the competitive aspects of chess, but also what he learned about himself and what mattered to him? What was important? Well, I mean, you, know, I thought this story that his father said hey, you've got to, you, know, what matters when you play a game is, you, know, just try your best. When I say to my son, I kind of mean it doesn't matter if you lose. What Demis heard from his dad was, you, know, I've got to try so hard, you, know, as you said, I'm going to, you, know, I'm going to exhaust myself, I'm going to kill myself, I'm going to, you, know, just everything, I have to give everything. And I heard this story initially from one of Demis's co-founders, Shane Legg. And, you, know, I sort of said is that really right? And he said oh yeah, there's no 50% mode in Demis, there's no 99% mode in Demis, it's always more than 100% all of the time. And I said what do you mean, like he has no hobbies? And he goes, well, maybe he might go to a game of the British soccer team, you, know, Liverpool, once in a while, but that's it. That's it. Otherwise, it's just the mission: build AI, build AI, build AI, build AI. And so yeah, I think I think chess created this incredibly competitive persona. Maybe also—and I kind of play with this idea a little bit—there were other reasons too. Some people if they can make a lot of friends, they don't need to prove themselves so much by winning competitions. And because he was weird in his school, right? Too clever, too Asian-looking, just different, very small physique, so he wasn't going to impress people or overpower them through strength. He needed to assert who he was in a different way and that was by winning games. And he won not only actually chess, he started to compete in other games and was the five-time winner of something called the "Mind Sports Olympiad." And, you, know, he took it incredibly seriously. I'd never heard of it before I met him, but anyway it was a big part of his life for a while just beating everybody at all these different games: backgammon, shogi, you, know, go, chess, there's a game called well I guess *Risk*. You, know, just lots of games. *Diplomacy*. I mentioned *Ender's Game* and Hofstadter's *Gödel, Escher, Bach* because, well for two reasons. One is that in the book you write about how Hassabis was very drawn to stories; he was a great storyteller. And a lot of his motivations and the way that he tells the story of what drives him are told in some ways indexed to certain stories or certain novels. And also his objectives in AI are not what you would call traditional entrepreneurial objectives. So like philosophy and eschatology and epistemology play a very big role in his motivations. What can you tell us about what motivates him on a deeper level and how this almost feels kind of like a spiritual or ontological pursuit for him? Yeah, well I mean he told me that he would stay up late, would always go to bed around 4:00 AM, and when he was sitting by himself reading, studying scientific papers, you, know, thinking about things at 4:00 AM, he would feel this sort of reality staring him in the face and saying, you, know, "here I am, I need to be understood." He had this sort of sense that to understand nature as a scientist was to draw closer to some maybe divine force that had created reality. He would say to me, "why is it that things are set up in the world in a way that we can study? Isn't it a coincidence?" And then he would sort of slam the table with his fist. "Isn't it a coincidence that this table is made of atoms jumping around, you, know, electrons, and yet it's solid and you can put your laptop on it? And that that laptop is made of bits of silicon and yet with electricity passing through, it can think? I mean, what's going on here Sebastian? Why is the world set up like this for it to work, for us to study? I need to understand why." "I need, I need. But before I die, before I die, I've got to understand this." And so that, yeah, it's just a really intense feeling for him that he has to understand, and I think that's why he fundamentally is building AI. And by the way, you, know, initially I thought wow, this is crazy, I've never met somebody so intense before. But then as I thought about it further over the next three years of talking to him, I realized that we all have an element where we look at technology—we love it, we want it—but at the same time we're frightened about what it's going to do to our society. And yet we move ahead. If we didn't take that trade of embracing a technology that's both exciting and scary, we would all still be living in caves. So in some sense, Demis is just like an enlargement of the human condition. That desire to understand, it's not just "I think therefore I am." It's "I invent therefore I am." And I think we all have some of that, it's just that Demis has it in very large scale. Well, there are a number of threads that I extracted from the book that I wrote down as notes as I was reading through it. And one of those threads had to do with the competitive dynamics of the industry. So you go to great lengths to show how these aren't decisions that are taken in isolation. It's not like Hassabis is sitting in a room and saying I have a choice to hit a button here, and if I don't hit the button nothing will happen, we won't get any progress in artificial intelligence, and if I do we might get a 50/50, you, know, existential extinction event. It's that everyone is competing with everyone else, and so the competitive dynamics almost drive inexorably toward innovation and whatever this outcome is going to be, whether it is cataclysmic or beneficial for humanity. How was that a dynamic that you feel like you were aware of early on? Did you gain more appreciation of for it as you sort of worked on the book, or did it really emerge through the course of writing it? I understood that there was a race dynamic the moment ChatGPT came out. And indeed I remember going to see Demis right after it came out. He said, "oh they've parked the tanks on our front garden, on our front lawn, you, know, this is war." There was a sense of betrayal too, wasn't there? A sense of sort of communal betrayal against what was sort of an unofficially agreed-upon consensus? Well, I mean, I'd say at least it's true that both DeepMind and also Anthropic had been working on a chatbot a bit like ChatGPT and they had been reluctant to release it because they weren't sure it wouldn't be toxic; they worried about hallucination and all that stuff. And, you, know, ChatGPT came out because OpenAI was less inhibited about those safety considerations. And so there was a sense that, you, know, this was not only wasn't a fair race, it was more like one side had just been opportunistic and unscrupulous in releasing ChatGPT. So there was that. And then, you, know, the other thing is that Microsoft, which of course was the major backer of OpenAI, didn't make any effort to conceal its glee that, you, know, ChatGPT had gotten ahead of its rival Google DeepMind. And Satya Nadella famously came out and said, you, know, "we've now overtaken them and they're going to have to show us if they can dance." Because, you, know, "can they dance?" is the challenge. Which was just—I know from my friends at Google—just super insulting and and infuriating. And so there was this—this was not—this was a "take the gloves off, bare-knuckle fight" kind of moment. And so that's when the race became totally clear, and that was at the beginning of my writing, so that was not a surprise to me. I think what was a surprise was understanding the backstory of how much Demis had tried to avoid this dynamic, this race to the bottom. Because he could only justify this quest for superhuman intelligence by telling himself that he would make it safe. It would be fantastic for advancing science, but it would also be safe. And so he had a number of theories about how to make it safe. And one of them was there would be a "singleton scenario." In other words, only one lab would develop AI on behalf of all humanity, and everybody would club together and make sure it was safe before it was released. And the moment when OpenAI was founded in 2015, which was five years after DeepMind, it was evident that that singleton scenario wasn't going to happen. And so that was just one out of several moments of disillusionment where a theory about how to make AI safe got blown up. And so part of the story here is like a sort of, you, know, an intelligent good person trying to make AI safe but theory after theory about how you would do that doesn't work. So let's use this opportunity to dig in a bit more into the foundations of the company. As you suggest, DeepMind was founded in 2010. Help me trace how Hassabis took this kernel of an idea that you mentioned in 1993 to build an AI company and turned it into a reality through the eventual founding of DeepMind along with his co-founders Shane Legg and Mustafa Suleyman in 2010. Well, right, so he had this idea he wanted to build AI before he got to college. Then he read computer science for three years at Cambridge. In the meantime he was still consulting on video game design with the guys he'd worked with before college. In fact, he persuaded the boss of of the company—it was called Bullfrog Games—and the Bullfrog boss gave him a really fast car, it was a Porsche 911. He would drive at crazy speeds from his campus to the to the gaming company in the middle of the night when there was no traffic and put in a couple of days of work with the gaming company and then come back and do classes again. So he was always juggling both computer science and kind of game design. When he got out of college, he worked a bit again with the same Bullfrog studio, and then he started his own game design company called Elixir Studios, which did okay. He made a bit of money but it, you, know, kind of eventually had to close it because it wasn't doing well enough. And then he was kind of burnt out at that point from, you, know, startups failing can be a pretty traumatic experience. And so both he and his co-founder David Silver, who is a big figure in my story because he was the scientist behind AlphaGo and various other systems, they both wanted to recuperate from entrepreneurship, so to relax they did PhDs. And David Silver went off and did a PhD in reinforcement learning in Canada. And Demis took a sort of more eccentric route: he did neuroscience. And the theory was, to build machine intelligence I need to understand how human intelligence works. If I understand, you, know, the kind of main existence proof that you can even have intelligence is the human mind. So if I do a neuroscience PhD, then I will understand the endpoint of where AI is trying to get to. He did this neuroscience PhD, was crazy good at it. He published a paper when he hadn't even finished his PhD which became one of the most cited neuroscience papers of that period. And then after that is when he founded DeepMind in 2010. So it's actually important to note now that we're talking about neuroscience that this was still in the period where symbolic approaches to AI were the norm, correct? Hmm. That's right. Yeah. I mean, so much programming had been kind of what we'd think of as logical deductive programming, you, know, "if A is bigger than B and B is bigger than C, then A is bigger than C also"—these kind of hard and fast logical rules. And the big jump in order to build AI was to go from deduction to induction. With induction there's no logic, there's just tons of examples. And you absorb all those examples and you kind of intuit the patterns from the examples. And that—that's induction. And the thing about induction is that if I was to study some examples of humanity, I would—let's say I went and looked at 10 different New Yorkers and I saw what they did in the morning—I would conclude that all humans drink coffee in the morning. But if I studied a million human beings and they were dispersed geographically, I would understand that no, they don't all drink coffee in the morning. You, know, some percentage do and some percentage, you, know, so any inductive conclusion is subject to revision when you have more examples. And so you need lots of examples, maybe even an infinity of examples in order to do good induction. And that's why I called the book *The Infinity Machine*, because this shift from deduction to induction only worked when you had a machine that could make sense of an infinity of data. It was also a way of conceding that we couldn't build superintelligence based off of our limited first-principle understanding of the universe. Yeah, and just that, you, know, we don't as humans—and this is where understanding how the human mind works was very important to understanding how AI was going to work—as humans, we don't think in terms of logic all the time. Most of what we do, you, know, some you know a face appears at the door and you recognize who the person is. There's no logic there, you just look and you recognize, right? And so, you, know, when I talk, I am not always grammatical. So even if you taught a machine the rules of human grammar, actually we as humans don't observe those rules very often. So just the whole idea that knowledge and communication could be decomposed into logical constructs is wrong. A lot of what we do and how we think is not actually logical. It's inductive, not deductive. And in fact this was, you, know, and you mentioned *Gödel, Escher, Bach*, the book that Demis read when he was a teenager. I mean, the most important thing about that book to Demis was really that Gödel, who is the mathematician in the title, Kurt Gödel, was most famous for what's called the incompleteness theorem. It shows that it's impossible to create a system of mathematical logic that completely explains everything that might be true in mathematics. So if it's not even possible for math, it's certainly not true for much messier emergent systems like, you, know, how our biology works. Or, you, know, look at the irregularities you find in nature. Bushes have all kinds of shapes and, you, know, irregular floors—I mean the ground is not even and it's just everything is super you, know, super irregular and it doesn't follow clear rules. If I say: why is a butter knife in the same category as a carving knife? One is small and blunt, the other is long and sharp. You would say, well, they're both knives meaning they both have the same function. But then if I say: why is a dachshund in the same category as a Labrador? They don't have the same function, they're both dogs, but what does that mean? Well, they're both the same species because they can mate together and have fertile offspring. But how do you know—how do you teach an AI that some categories of objects we classify according to their reproductive potential, others by their function, others by their color? I mean there is no rule that describes that. And so just the point being that logic is super limited. And so AI was a way of developing software systems that could think inductively, not deductively. Do you think that the limitations of formal systems and the inherent contradictions within them stem from the limitations of language? And that and that that shows up, for example, in visual recognition? Like how do you define that something is a cat? You know, it could be a small furry animal with whiskers, but it could be a raccoon. Or how do you formalize that into a language that computers can understand from first principles? And is that a limitation of our language, do you think? So you can't formalize it into a language that a computer will understand from first principles, which is why both facial recognition and language recognition have required AI systems that don't think in terms of first-order logic. They are way more subtle and complex than that. And, you, know, the way that they can recognize a cat is they use these neural networks which, you, know, have thousands of decision centers that are picking up on very subtle relationships between, okay, these two bits of black in the picture are a continuous line that delineates an eye and then there's a pupil in the eye. I mean, it's just a much more subtle process which is learned through trial and error. So you show the system a labeled picture of a cat and then you mix up the cat's pictures with non-cats—it could be, you, know, tigers or furry blankets or something that you might mix up with a cat—and the system tries to guess what's a cat. And if it gets the answer wrong, then it will tweak its own internal code, its parameters, so that it gets the next time it answers slightly better. And through trial and error, it lands on a set of parameters, on a code basically—on a code that unlocks the trick of recognizing a cat. And the programmer, the human programmer, doesn't know how that worked. It's the machine that has discovered through trial and error how it works. And so this is—you, know, it is an illustration of the limits of logic. So how does this relate to Hassabis's early skepticism of language models and their limitations? Why did he feel that that was not the path to general artificial intelligence? Yeah, this is very interesting and important. I mean, there was this moment in 2017 where the transformer paper came out, which was this scientific breakthrough in how you find patterns in long sequences of data. And the one clear example of this is long text, right? Sentences and paragraphs that go on and on. And that was a kind of unsolved problem in computer science: how do you have a good translation machine or a good language recognition machine? And 2017, this paper comes out from Google Research and it's a huge breakthrough. Immediately, on the very day that paper comes out, the chief scientist at OpenAI, Ilya Sutskever, tells me he jumps out of his chair, runs down the corridor, finds his collaborator Alec Radford and says, "you, know, drop what you're doing, we're going to do this, we're going to apply this transformer thing, we're going to apply it to language." Meanwhile, over at the rival lab DeepMind, Demis is like: nothing to see here, I'm not interested in pursuing language. And, you, know, he had a few people vaguely working on language in DeepMind and, you, know, Demis says to me, "oh you're being unfair, you, know, we did have a language team." But frankly he didn't focus on this language team. The language team they did have was not really doing the right kind of research to make use of the transformer. And so it was sort of just a missing piece of DeepMind's agenda at the time. They were more interested in these trial and error systems that were playing games. They'd done AlphaGo, they'd beaten the human Go champion, so they were interested in agentic systems that played games, not in language. And the reason was that partly that Demis felt that language was not enough to get to intelligence. Language is a system of symbols, and mapping symbols onto some other symbols, for example pictures—so you have the word "cat," then you have a picture of a cat, you map one signal onto the other—how is that connected to reality? was what Demis was thinking. You, know, you need to be grounded in the real world to be properly intelligent, and that was his thought. And, you, know, just language ain't going to get you there. And it took about three years, between 2017 and 2020, for Demis to change his mind on that. And the tipping point came in the summer of 2020 when OpenAI released GPT-3, the third iteration of its language model. And that was just way too good to ignore. Really, it did seem that a language-trained system could conduct highly intelligent, cogent conversation. And so that was the beginning of DeepMind waking up and racing to catch up. So I'd like to at least cover the Google acquisition before we move to the second hour, Sebastian. That acquisition by Google happened in 2014, and it gave DeepMind essentially unlimited resources. But it embedded it inside of a global corporation with its own priorities, its own culture, and an incentive structure that could tend to view the kind of technological disruption that DeepMind was pursuing as inherently threatening to the company's core business. What informed Hassabis's decision and the decision of the team? And what do we know about how aligned the co-founders were, as well as some of the early investors, in proceeding with the acquisition? Well, one of the things I discovered is that Demis was such a dominant founder that the other two couldn't really challenge what he wanted to do. So it was all on Demis. I mean, he had at the founding 90% of the shares and the rest of the shares were with the other two guys. And so he was dominant. And for him, the decision was really, you, know, dealing with raising money from venture capitalists was taking up a huge amount of his time. It was taking him away from the scientific research he wanted to focus on. And at the same time, the amount of money he needed was starting to go up fast because it was just the beginning of, you, know, deep neural networks getting deeper. And so you needed more chips, more hardware, and you needed to compete to hire the best scientists because other labs were now trying to hire them as well. And so, you, know, he needed money, and the only question was: would he sell himself to Google or would he sell himself to Meta or some other company? And he actually went to see Mark Zuckerberg—I guess it was Facebook then, not Meta—and talked to Zuckerberg and he had this amusing test that he administered on him where he said, "you, know, I'm working on AI as you know, and I think it's super important." And Mark Zuckerberg said, "yes, yes, very, very important." And so then Demis carried on having dinner and towards the end of the dinner he mentions how great augmented reality is as a technology. And Mark Zuckerberg says, "yeah, that's fascinating and we're really going to invest big in that one." And then Demis a bit later mentioned something else—I don't know what it is, artificial reality or some other kind of technology—and again Zuckerberg is like, "yeah, yeah, that's great, we're going to do that." And Demis thought, "okay, I understand this guy." "He says everything is great, he doesn't really mean it about AI, he doesn't get that AI is just way more important than augmented reality, and if he doesn't get that I'm not selling to him." So then he pivots back to Google and he does the deal with Google. He has to fend off Elon Musk, who is very keen to buy the company, hates Larry Page of Google at that moment, and wants to kind of compete with Larry Page by putting DeepMind inside SpaceX or Tesla. And Demis says, "no, no, no," he doesn't want to do that. So he goes with Google. And look, it worked out incredibly well because Google was a good parent company; it did give Demis tons of resources to do compute and to hire great scientists. And so he made the right call. You know, I love this story because it shows both how perceptive he is. How unusually perceptive he is about people. And yet in the book you also show how he could also be incredibly naive. What explains that—this dual character in Hassabis? I think he was naive about how Silicon Valley worked because he wasn't based there. He was based in London. He's quite patriotically British. He believes that the social democratic values of Britain, you know, mean a lot to him. If you go to Silicon Valley, it's quite hard to find any public space. If you go to the DeepMind office in King's Cross in London, there are, you know, barefooted kids playing soccer in the summer who come from the local government housing and they're playing on the government—on the government, you know, soccer pitch. And so it's just a different vibe and Demis cares about that. So I think he was naive in terms of how to play the venture capital raising game. He believed too much in Peter Thiel, who was the Series A financier, and he thought that Peter Thiel would, you know, give him more capital and in fact he was wrong about that. Peter was quite reluctant. He's such a contrarian that he invested at the beginning when nothing was working in AI, and then when it starts to work, Peter Thiel's like, "hmm, I think it's probably overheating now, it's time to sell." He's always against the consensus, which I find hilarious. But anyway, that's his Girardian—that's his Gir- the Girardian him. He certainly is an interesting thinker. In any event, I think, you know, clearly the venture capital game is something that Demis was naive at. And then also I'd say the inevitability of competition amongst multiple different AI labs is something he underestimated. I mean, the notion that there would be a singleton lab, like one lab building AI on behalf of all reality—on behalf of all humanity—obviously underestimates the truth that if you've got something as exciting as AI, multiple labs with multiple ambitious entrepreneurs in multiple countries are going to come after this goal. It's just too rich, too sweet not to chase. In retrospect, do you feel like Demis thinks it was the right decision to sell to Google or do you think there's some regret there? Well, now and again he would say to me, "Oh, you know, if only I'd been in that position trying to raise money and it had been not 2013, but 2015, I could have just raised my money independently, I could have remained independent." But you know, I don't—I think that's like a sort of—that's the minority share of his mind, right? The majority of the time he says, you know, it worked out great, I got all the money I needed. I had a choice in my career: did I want to build an independent company and build it into a Google, or did I want to use the resources of Google to build towards artificial general intelligence and do great science? And he has no doubt that he cares much more about the science than about company building. He's quite good at company building, but his primary objective is science. And so when he got the Nobel Prize, that was way bigger for him than it would be to, you know, be a ten times billionaire or something. So, Sebastian, I'm going to move us to the second hour. I think where I'd like to pick off is, or where I'd like to pick up there is ChatGPT 3.5, the launch in late November of 2022, and this also coincides with when you first went to him to pitch him on this project. Inside the Iran War with Steven Cook: What’s REALLY Happening? | The Real Eisman Playbook Ep 52

Steve Eisman(165.0K) ·40:17 ·53.8K views · 2026-03-30
Steve Eisman (Host, The Real Eisman Playbook) Steven Cook (Middle East Expert)
The U.S.
TL;DR The U.S. and Israel have achieved significant tactical successes in degrading Iran’s military and nuclear capabilities, but the strategic outcome remains highly uncertain as President Trump maintains vague war aims to preserve negotiation leverage. While Iran’s proxies are...

TL;DR

The U.S. and Israel have achieved significant tactical successes in degrading Iran’s military and nuclear capabilities, but the strategic outcome remains highly uncertain as President Trump maintains vague war aims to preserve negotiation leverage. While Iran’s proxies are currently suppressed and Gulf allies seek a definitive end to the threat, the conflict risks ending in a "messy middle" where the regime survives and regional stability remains elusive.

Current Military Status and Tactical Gains

  • Degradation of Capabilities: Expert Steven Cook notes that the U.S. and Israel have successfully set back Iran’s missile, drone, and nuclear programs. Iran’s missile manufacturing, previously at 200 units per month, has been severely crippled.
  • Proxy Suppression: Iran’s "Ring of Fire" (Hezbollah, Houthis, and Iraqi militias) appears intimidated. Notably, the Lebanese government is showing an unprecedented desire to see the end of Hezbollah's influence.
  • U.S.-Israeli Integration: In a historic shift, the two nations are operating as a single unit under Central Command (CENTCOM), with pilots sharing airbases and officers divvying up targets in the same planning cells.
  • The Five-Day Pause: President Trump has implemented a temporary hiatus on bombing Iranian oil infrastructure, citing ongoing talks. Cook remains skeptical of these negotiations, suggesting Iran is unlikely to concede on core issues like the Strait of Hormuz.

Regional Response and Arab State Dynamics

  • Shift in Gulf Sentiment: While Arab states (UAE, Saudi Arabia, Qatar) initially feared chaos, Iran’s retaliatory strikes against civilian and oil infrastructure have unified them against Tehran.
  • The "Finish It" Mandate: Gulf partners, heavily invested in trillion-dollar domestic transformations (e.g., Saudi Vision 2030), now prefer the U.S. and Israel "finish" the job rather than allow a premature end that leaves them vulnerable.
  • Hardening Defenses: Regardless of the outcome, Cook predicts a massive regional investment in "hardening" cities, including the construction of bomb shelters and anti-drone systems in previously untouched hubs like Dubai and Riyadh.
  • The "Big Hedge": Over the last decade, perceived U.S. withdrawal from the Middle East led Arab states to "hedge" their bets by strengthening ties with China.

Global Influence: Russia and China

  • Russian Complicity: Russia is actively providing Iran with real-time satellite intelligence and targeting data for U.S. and Israeli installations. Cook describes this as direct "payback" for U.S. assistance to Ukraine.
  • U.S. Oil Sanctions Paradox: In a controversial move to keep domestic gas prices low, the Trump administration has eased some restrictions on Russian and Iranian oil flow to increase global supply.
  • China’s Stability Focus: China prioritizes its relationship with Saudi Arabia over Iran. While they want the region stable for energy flow, they are unwilling to get "wrapped around the axle" of the conflict and will likely remain on the diplomatic sidelines.

Forecast: The "Messy Middle" and Internal Dissent

  • Three-Month Outlook: Cook predicts a "messy middle" scenario where the Islamic Republic survives in a weakened state, necessitating a long-term, high-intensity U.S. military presence to maintain containment.
  • Domestic Opposition: The Iranian population remains 90% opposed to the regime, with upwards of 30,000 civilians reportedly killed in recent crackdowns. While the "fear factor" keeps them indoors during active bombing, waves of future protest remain a high probability.
  • Nuclear Constraints: A key strategic risk is the cache of highly enriched uranium buried at sites like Isfahan. Cook argues that any ceasefire without verifiable destruction of this material would render the entire military campaign "for naught."
  • Trump's Leverage Strategy: By keeping war aims (regime change vs. degradation) vague, Trump maintains the flexibility to declare victory at any point or pivot to a "deal" without being boxed in by specific milestones.

Notable Quotes

"It is genuinely unprecedented for the United States and Israel to be operating like this. If you told me 10 years ago... that Israeli and American pilots were flying from the same air bases in Israel to attack Iran, I would say that was crazy." — Steven Cook
"The nightmare scenario for America's Gulf partners is a premature end to the war that leaves them vulnerable to the Iranians. Basically, what they've said is, 'We didn't want this war, but now that you've started it, finish it.'" — Steven Cook
"Trump has intentionally left the war aims vague. He's a deal guy and he wants as much maneuverability as possible... if he leaves the war aims vague, he can maneuver as much as possible." — Steve Eisman
"I think people have been really surprised at the resilience of the regime. I think that they have suffered... but they still clearly have some fight left in them." — Steven Cook

Exclusive: Rubio speaks on Iran war and America’s next move | This Is America

Al Jazeera English(17.8M) ·52:27 ·57.5K views · 2026-03-30
> "The Iranian regime can never have nuclear weapons...
TL;DR: Secretary of State Marco Rubio asserts that the U.S. military operation against Iran will conclude within "weeks, not months," focusing on the total neutralization of Iran's nuclear ambitions and missile infrastructure while signaling a potential "re-examination" of NATO...

TL;DR: Secretary of State Marco Rubio asserts that the U.S. military operation against Iran will conclude within "weeks, not months," focusing on the total neutralization of Iran's nuclear ambitions and missile infrastructure while signaling a potential "re-examination" of NATO due to lack of allied support.

Military Objectives and Timeline

  • Primary Goals: The administration's mission is defined by three pillars: ensuring the Iranian regime never obtains a nuclear weapon, ending their sponsorship of terrorism, and dismantling their ability to threaten neighbors with missile technology.
  • Progress Report: Rubio claims the U.S. has already achieved significant military milestones:
      • Air and Sea: The Iranian Navy and Air Force have been largely destroyed.
      • Infrastructure: The U.S. is currently "well on its way" to destroying arms factories and significantly reducing the number of active missile launchers.
  • The "Weeks, Not Months" Timeline: Rubio explicitly stated the operation is not intended to last months. He expects the military objectives to be met in a matter of weeks, though he declined to provide a specific end date.
  • Operational Scope: While Rubio spoke of the "incredible people" of Iran, he clarified that "regime change" is not the formal objective of the current operation, though the U.S. would "facilitate" or "welcome" a change in leadership.

Diplomacy and Conditions for Ceasefire

  • Minimum Concessions: For hostilities to end, Tehran must take "demonstrable steps" to abandon nuclear ambitions. Rubio suggests Iran adopt the model used by other regional powers: importing fuel for energy rather than maintaining domestic enrichment and weaponization capabilities.
  • Weapon Programs: Iran must abandon its long-range missile and drone programs. Rubio highlighted recent "lies" from the Iranian Foreign Ministry regarding the range of their missiles as proof that the regime cannot be trusted.
  • Communication Channels: Rubio confirmed that some direct and indirect talks (through intermediaries like Pakistan) are occurring, noting that President Trump "always prefers diplomacy" if it can achieve the necessary outcomes.

Strategic Pressure and Regional Security

  • The Strait of Hormuz: Reopening this critical waterway is a top priority. Rubio stated it will be opened either through a diplomatic deal or by force.
      • Escalation Risks: Analysts suggest the U.S. may consider seizing Khark Island to deny Iran oil revenue and ensure safe passage for global shipping.
  • The "Imminent Threat" Justification: Rubio defended the preemptive nature of the war, citing Iran’s 60% uranium enrichment and stockpiles as a "tolerable risk" that had to be addressed before they reached 90% (weapons-grade) enrichment.
  • Regional Defense: Rubio emphasized that Iranian missiles are specifically designed to target Saudi Arabia, the UAE, Qatar, Kuwait, and Bahrain. He vowed that the U.S. will continue to support these allies against the current "onslaught."

Allied Relations and Financial Costs

  • NATO Strains: Rubio expressed sharp disappointment with NATO allies, specifically naming Spain for denying the U.S. use of its airspace and bases during the conflict.
      • Policy Shift: Rubio warned that if NATO is only about the U.S. defending Europe without receiving reciprocal basing rights for U.S. national interests, the relationship will need to be "re-examined."
  • Burden Sharing: White House Press Secretary Karine Jean-Pierre indicated that President Trump expects Gulf allies to contribute financially to the costs of the war.
      • Diplomatic Friction: Experts noted this may be a "tough swallow" for Gulf nations that have already been the targets of Iranian retaliatory strikes.
"The Iranian regime can never have nuclear weapons... The United States is committed to finishing the job. The Strait of Hormuz will be open. When this operation is over, it will be open." — Marco Rubio, US Secretary of State
"When those objectives are achieved... that's something that's not going to take months. That's a matter of weeks. I'm not going to tell you exactly how many weeks, but a matter of weeks, not months." — Marco Rubio, US Secretary of State
"If NATO is just about us defending Europe if they're attacked, but them denying us basing rights when we need them, that's not a very good arrangement. That's a hard one to stay engaged in." — Marco Rubio, US Secretary of State

"Well, there's messages and some direct talks going on between some inside of Iran and the United States, primarily through intermediaries, but there's been some conversation. So they wouldn't have water shortages, so their economies would provide opportunities for an incredible people. The people of Iran are incredible people, very resourceful, very entrepreneurial, but it's their regime that's been a problem. So we would always welcome a scenario in which Iran was led by people that had a different view of the future and a different view, and if that opportunity presents itself, we're going to take it." "Iran can never, the Iranian regime can never have nuclear weapons and they need to stop sponsoring terrorism and they need to stop building weapons that can threaten their neighbors. These short-range missiles that they're launching, they only have one purpose and that is to attack Saudi Arabia and the UAE and Qatar and Kuwait and Bahrain. That is the range of these weapons. And they have thousands of them. They've built extraordinary numbers of them. And not to mention, you know, their desire to have this nuclear weapon to threaten and blackmail the world with. That will never be allowed to happen. It's too great a risk." What would you consider to be the minimum concession from Tehran to bring this conflict to an end? "I think they have to take demonstrable steps towards ending any ambition of having nuclear weapons. If what they want is nuclear energy, there are multiple countries in the region that have or will soon have nuclear energy and they're going to do it through a well-established mechanism that everyone else has agreed to. And that is that you allow imported fuel to come. You import the fuel, and that fuel is used to power up your power grid and provide energy resources for your people. What they cannot have is a system that allows them to quickly weaponize it. They have to stop making all of these drones and all of these missiles. We saw recently where they've lied. They said they didn't have long-range missiles and then they fired two that extended well beyond the range of what their own foreign minister just a few days earlier said they didn't have the range to reach. So they have to abandon all these weapon programs and all of their nuclear ambitions, and if they do that then Iran's future can be bright. But they have to choose that path and for 47 years they've refused that path." The role of Secretary of State is, of course, run out of the State Department. It leads America's foreign affairs. It allows us to station troops and aircraft and weapons in parts of the world that we wouldn't normally have bases and that includes in much of Europe. And to see that in a time of need the United States has identified a grave risk to our national security and our national interests and we needed to conduct this operation and we have countries like Spain, a NATO member that we are pledged to defend, denying us the use of their airspace and bragging about it, denying us the use of their bases. And there are other countries that have done that as well. And so you ask yourself well what is in it for the United States? And I've been a big supporter of NATO and one of the reasons why I've been a supporter of NATO is because I believe that these basing rights give us leverage and give us flexibility and operational capability all over the world. But if NATO is just about us defending Europe if they're attacked but them denying us basing rights when we need them, that's not a very good arrangement. That's a hard one to stay engaged in. President Trump Gaggles with Press on Air Force One En Route Joint Base Andrews, Mar. 29, 2026

The White House(3.0M) ·20:47 ·196.3K views · 2026-03-30
President Trump announced a "regime change" in Iran following the destruction of their Navy and Air Force, while simultaneously unveiling designs for a privately funded White House ballroom and demanding the Senate terminate the filibuster to pass the "Save America Act.
TL;DR President Trump announced a "regime change" in Iran following the destruction of their Navy and Air Force, while simultaneously unveiling designs for a privately funded White House ballroom and demanding the Senate terminate the filibuster to pass the "Save America Act." ...

TL;DR

President Trump announced a "regime change" in Iran following the destruction of their Navy and Air Force, while simultaneously unveiling designs for a privately funded White House ballroom and demanding the Senate terminate the filibuster to pass the "Save America Act."

Diplomatic Breakthrough and Military Action in Iran

  • Military Status: President Trump claimed the U.S. has successfully "decimated" the Iranian military, specifically noting the destruction of 158 ships (their entire Navy), their Air Force, and the majority of their missile capabilities.
  • Negotiations and "Tribute": The President reported direct and indirect negotiations with a "third regime" of Iranian leadership that he described as "much more reasonable."
      • Oil Shipments: As a sign of respect or "tribute," Iran has reportedly agreed to send 30 massive boatloads of oil through the Strait of Hormuz (10 initially, followed by an additional 20).
  • Regime Change: Trump asserted that de facto regime change has occurred because the first two tiers of Iranian leadership are "all dead" or incapacitated, leaving a new group to negotiate.
  • Nuclear Stance: The primary objective remains ensuring Iran never acquires a nuclear weapon, with the President suggesting a deal could be reached "very soon."

White House Ballroom and Subterranean Complex

  • Project Details: Trump presented architectural plans for a new White House ballroom, designed to be the "finest of its kind in the world."
      • Design: The structure matches the height of the White House, features hand-carved Corinthian columns, and replaces the south-side stairs with an open porch overlooking the National Mall.
      • Security: The building is fully reinforced with bulletproof glass and "drone-proof" roofs and ceilings.
  • Funding: The President emphasized that "not one dime of government money" is being used for the ballroom; it is entirely funded by Trump himself and private donors.
  • Military Infrastructure: A "massive complex" for the military is currently under construction beneath the ballroom. Trump described the ballroom itself as a "shed" or cover for this underlying military facility.

Legislative Strategy and the "Save America Act"

  • Filibuster Termination: Trump called on Senate Republicans—specifically Leader John Thune—to "terminate the filibuster" to pass key legislation. He dismissed Thune’s concerns about lacking the votes, stating, "that’s what being a leader is, you have to get the votes."
  • The "Save America Act": The President insisted on the 5-point plan, which includes:
      • Election Integrity: Universal voter ID, proof of citizenship for voting, and the elimination of most mail-in ballots.
      • Social Policy: Federal bans on "men playing in women's sports" and "transgender mutilation" of children.
  • DHS and ICE Funding: Trump criticized Senate Democrats, labeling them "terrorists" for their stance on border funding. He reaffirmed that ICE is "vital" and will not sign a deal that excludes their funding.

Regional Foreign Policy: Cuba and the Middle East

  • Cuba: The President predicted that Cuba is a "failing country" and will be the "next" regime to collapse. He stated he has no objection to Russian oil tankers delivering fuel to Cuba in the interim because "the people need heat and cooling."
  • Gulf Allies: Trump reported that Saudi Arabia, Qatar, UAE, Kuwait, and Bahrain are "fighting back hard" against Iranian influence and are "100% on our side."
  • Personnel: Trump expressed continued confidence in Tulsi Gabbard despite her being "softer" on Iran, and indicated he may reconsider the nomination of Casey Beames for Surgeon General in favor of other options.

***

Notable Quotes
